      <p>
       <b>
        Portuguese
       </b>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="pt">
         <i>
          português
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       or, in full,
       <i>
        <span lang="pt">
         <i>
          língua portuguesa
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       ) is a
       <a href="/wiki/Western_Romance_languages" title="Western Romance languages">
        West Romance language
       </a>
       and the sole official language of
       <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">
        Portugal
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">
        Cape Verde
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Guinea-Bissau" title="Guinea-Bissau">
        Guinea-Bissau
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">
        Mozambique
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">
        Angola
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Tom%C3%A9_and_Pr%C3%ADncipe" title="São Tomé and Príncipe">
        São Tomé and Príncipe
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPLP_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CPLP-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It also has co-official language status in
       <a href="/wiki/East_Timor" title="East Timor">
        East Timor
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_Guinea" title="Equatorial Guinea">
        Equatorial Guinea
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">
        Macau
       </a>
       in China. As the result of expansion during colonial times, a cultural presence of Portuguese and
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese-based_creole_languages" title="Portuguese-based creole languages">
        Portuguese creole
       </a>
       speakers are also found in
       <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">
        Goa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Daman_and_Diu" title="Daman and Diu">
        Daman and Diu
       </a>
       in India;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Batticaloa" title="Batticaloa">
        Batticaloa
       </a>
       on the east coast of
       <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">
        Sri Lanka
       </a>
       ; in the
       <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">
        Indonesian
       </a>
       island of
       <a href="/wiki/Flores" title="Flores">
        Flores
       </a>
       ; in the
       <a href="/wiki/Malacca" title="Malacca">
        Malacca
       </a>
       state of
       <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">
        Malaysia
       </a>
       ; and the
       <a href="/wiki/ABC_islands_(Lesser_Antilles)" title="ABC islands (Lesser Antilles)">
        ABC islands
       </a>
       in the Caribbean where
       <a href="/wiki/Papiamento" title="Papiamento">
        Papiamento
       </a>
       is spoken, while
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verdean_Creole" title="Cape Verdean Creole">
        Cape Verdean Creole
       </a>
       is the most widely spoken Portuguese-based Creole. A Portuguese-speaking person or nation may be referred to as "
       <a href="/wiki/Lusophone" title="Lusophone">
        Lusophone
       </a>
       " in both English and Portuguese.
      </p>
      <p>
       Portuguese is part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Romance_languages" title="Iberian Romance languages">
        Ibero-Romance group
       </a>
       that evolved from several dialects of
       <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">
        Vulgar Latin
       </a>
       in the medieval
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Galicia" title="Kingdom of Galicia">
        Kingdom of Galicia
       </a>
       , and has kept some
       <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">
        Celtic
       </a>
       phonology and lexicon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With approximately 215 to 220 million native speakers and 260 million total speakers, Portuguese is usually listed as the sixth
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="List of languages by number of native speakers">
        most natively spoken language
       </a>
       in the world, the third-most spoken European language in the world in terms of native speakers,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and a major language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">
        Southern Hemisphere
       </a>
       . It is also the most spoken language in South America and the second-most spoken in Latin America after Spanish, and is an official language of the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercosul" title="Mercosul">
        Mercosul
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_American_States#Official_languages" title="Organization of American States">
        OAS
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_West_African_States" title="Economic Community of West African States">
        ECOWAS
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">
        African Union
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Portuguese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Portuguese_language" title="History of the Portuguese language">
        History of the Portuguese language
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       When the Romans arrived in the
       <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">
        Iberian Peninsula
       </a>
       in 216 B.C., they brought the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">
        Latin language
       </a>
       with them, from which all Romance languages descend. The language was spread by Roman soldiers, settlers, and merchants, who built
       <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">
        Roman
       </a>
       cities mostly near the settlements of previous
       <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">
        Celtic
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Celtiberians" title="Celtiberians">
        Celtiberian
       </a>
       civilizations established long before the Roman arrivals.
      </p>
      <p>
       Between 409 A.D. and 711 A.D., as the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
        Roman Empire
       </a>
       collapsed in
       <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">
        Western Europe
       </a>
       , the Iberian Peninsula was conquered by
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">
        Germanic peoples
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">
        Migration Period
       </a>
       . The occupiers, mainly
       <a href="/wiki/Suebi" title="Suebi">
        Suebi
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">
        Visigoths
       </a>
       who originally spoke
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic languages
       </a>
       , quickly adopted late Roman culture and the
       <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">
        Vulgar Latin
       </a>
       dialects of the peninsula and over the next 300 years totally integrated into the local populations. After the
       <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">
        Moorish
       </a>
       invasion beginning in 711,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
        Arabic
       </a>
       became the administrative and common language in the conquered regions, but most of the
       <a href="/wiki/Mozarabs" title="Mozarabs">
        remaining Christian population
       </a>
       continued to speak a form of
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance
       </a>
       commonly known as
       <a href="/wiki/Mozarabic_language" title="Mozarabic language">
        Mozarabic
       </a>
       , which lasted three centuries longer in
       <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
        Spain
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Portuguese evolved from the medieval language, known today by linguists as
       <a href="/wiki/Galician-Portuguese" title="Galician-Portuguese">
        Galician-Portuguese
       </a>
       , Old Portuguese or Old Galician, of the northwestern medieval
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Galicia" title="Kingdom of Galicia">
        Kingdom of Galicia
       </a>
       . It is in Latin administrative documents of the 9th century that written Galician-Portuguese words and phrases are first recorded. This phase is known as Proto-Portuguese, which lasted from the 9th century until the 12th-century independence of the
       <a href="/wiki/County_of_Portugal" title="County of Portugal">
        County of Portugal
       </a>
       from the Kingdom of León, by then reigning over Galicia.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the first part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Galician-Portuguese" title="Galician-Portuguese">
        Galician-Portuguese
       </a>
       period (from the 12th to the 14th century), the language was increasingly used for documents and other written forms. For some time, it was the language of preference for
       <a href="/wiki/Lyric_poetry" title="Lyric poetry">
        lyric poetry
       </a>
       in Christian
       <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">
        Hispania
       </a>
       , much as
       <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">
        Occitan
       </a>
       was the language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Occitan_literature#Poetry_of_the_troubadours" title="Occitan literature">
        poetry of the troubadours
       </a>
       in France. Portugal became an independent kingdom in 1139, under King
       <a href="/wiki/Afonso_I_of_Portugal" title="Afonso I of Portugal">
        Afonso I of Portugal
       </a>
       . In 1290, King
       <a href="/wiki/Denis_of_Portugal" title="Denis of Portugal">
        Denis of Portugal
       </a>
       created the first Portuguese university in Lisbon (the
       <i>
        Estudos Gerais
       </i>
       , which later moved to
       <a href="/wiki/Coimbra" title="Coimbra">
        Coimbra
       </a>
       ) and decreed for Portuguese, then simply called the "common language", to be known as the Portuguese language and used officially.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the second period of Old Portuguese, in the 15th and 16th centuries, with the
       <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">
        Portuguese discoveries
       </a>
       , the language was taken to many regions of Africa, Asia, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">
        Americas
       </a>
       . By the mid-16th century, Portuguese had become a
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
         lingua franca
        </a>
       </i>
       in Asia and Africa, used not only for colonial administration and trade but also for communication between local officials and Europeans of all nationalities.
      </p>
      <p>
       Its spread was helped by mixed marriages between Portuguese and local people and by its association with
       <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
        Roman Catholic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">
        missionary
       </a>
       efforts, which led to the formation of
       <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">
        creole languages
       </a>
       such as that called
       <a href="/wiki/Kristang_language" title="Kristang language">
        Kristang
       </a>
       in many parts of Asia (from the word
       <i>
        cristão
       </i>
       , "Christian"). The language continued to be popular in parts of Asia until the 19th century. Some Portuguese-speaking Christian communities in
       <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
        India
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">
        Sri Lanka
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">
        Malaysia
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">
        Indonesia
       </a>
       preserved their language even after they were isolated from Portugal.
      </p>
      <p>
       The end of the Old Portuguese period was marked by the publication of the
       <i>
        Cancioneiro Geral
       </i>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Garcia_de_Resende" title="Garcia de Resende">
        Garcia de Resende
       </a>
       , in 1516. The early times of Modern Portuguese, which spans the period from the 16th century to the present day, were characterized by an increase in the number of learned words borrowed from
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">
        Classical Latin
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">
        Classical Greek
       </a>
       because of the
       <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
        Renaissance
       </a>
       (learned words borrowed from Latin also came from
       <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_Latin" title="Renaissance Latin">
        Renaissance Latin
       </a>
       , the form of Latin during that time), which greatly enriched the lexicon. Most literate Portuguese speakers were also literate in Latin; and thus they easily adopted Latin words into their writing—and eventually speech—in Portuguese.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Spanish author
       <a href="/wiki/Miguel_de_Cervantes" title="Miguel de Cervantes">
        Miguel de Cervantes
       </a>
       once called Portuguese "the sweet and gracious language", while the Brazilian poet
       <a href="/wiki/Olavo_Bilac" title="Olavo Bilac">
        Olavo Bilac
       </a>
       described it as
       <i>
        <span lang="pt">
         <i>
          a última flor do Lácio, inculta e bela
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       ("the last flower of
       <a href="/wiki/Latium" title="Latium">
        Latium
       </a>
       , rustic and beautiful"). Portuguese is also termed "the language of Camões", after
       <a href="/wiki/Lu%C3%ADs_de_Cam%C3%B5es" title="Luís de Camões">
        Luís Vaz de Camões
       </a>
       , one of the greatest literary figures in the Portuguese language and author of the Portuguese
       <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">
        epic poem
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Os_Lus%C3%ADadas" title="Os Lusíadas">
         Os Lusíadas
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In March 2006, the
       <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_the_Portuguese_Language" title="Museum of the Portuguese Language">
        Museum of the Portuguese Language
       </a>
       , an interactive museum about the Portuguese language, was founded in
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">
        São Paulo
       </a>
       , Brazil, the city with the greatest number of Portuguese language speakers in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOVA_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NOVA-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The museum is the first of its kind in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOVA_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NOVA-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2015 the museum was destroyed in a fire, but there are plans to reconstruct it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">
        Geographic distribution
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Portuguese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Multilingual_Emergency_Assembly_Area_Sign_in_Oizumi.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Multilingual_Emergency_Assembly_Area_Sign_in_Oizumi.JPG/220px-Multilingual_Emergency_Assembly_Area_Sign_in_Oizumi.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Multilingual_Emergency_Assembly_Area_Sign_in_Oizumi.JPG/330px-Multilingual_Emergency_Assembly_Area_Sign_in_Oizumi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Multilingual_Emergency_Assembly_Area_Sign_in_Oizumi.JPG/440px-Multilingual_Emergency_Assembly_Area_Sign_in_Oizumi.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Multilingual_Emergency_Assembly_Area_Sign_in_Oizumi.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Multilingual sign in Japanese, Portuguese, and English in
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oizumi" title="Oizumi">
          Oizumi
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">
          Japan
         </a>
         . Return immigration of
         <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Brazilians" title="Japanese Brazilians">
          Japanese Brazilians
         </a>
         has led to a large Portuguese-speaking community in the town.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
          <a href="#cite_note-17">
           [17]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographic_distribution_of_Portuguese" title="Geographic distribution of Portuguese">
        Geographic distribution of Portuguese
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Portuguese is the native language of the vast majority of people in Brazil
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and Portugal,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Special_Eurobarometer_243_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Special_Eurobarometer_243-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and 99.8% of the population of São Tomé and Príncipe declared speaking Portuguese in the 1991 census.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Perhaps 75% of the population of Angola speaks Portuguese,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and 85% are more or less fluent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Just over 40% (and rapidly increasing) of the population of Mozambique are native speakers of Portuguese, and 60% are fluent, according to the 2007 census.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Portuguese is also spoken natively by 30% of the population in Guinea-Bissau, and a Portuguese-based creole is understood by all.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       No data is available for Cape Verde, but almost all the population is bilingual, and the monolingual population speaks
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verdean_Creole" title="Cape Verdean Creole">
        Cape Verdean Creole
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       There are also significant Portuguese speaking immigrant communities in many countries including
       <a href="/wiki/Andorra" title="Andorra">
        Andorra
       </a>
       (15.4%),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">
        Bermuda
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
        Canada
       </a>
       (400,275 people in the 2006 census),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        France
       </a>
       (900,000 people),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">
        Japan
       </a>
       (400,000 people),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">
        Jersey
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">
        Namibia
       </a>
       (about 4–5% of the population, mainly refugees from Angola in the North of the country),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.namibian.com.na_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-www.namibian.com.na-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">
        Paraguay
       </a>
       (10.7% or 636,000 people),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">
        Macau
       </a>
       (0.6% or 12,000 people),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">
        Switzerland
       </a>
       (196,000 nationals in 2008),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">
        Venezuela
       </a>
       (554,000).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the United States (0.35% of the population or 1,228,126 speakers according to the 2007
       <a href="/wiki/American_Community_Survey" title="American Community Survey">
        American Community Survey
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In some parts of former
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_India" title="Portuguese India">
        Portuguese India
       </a>
       , namely
       <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">
        Goa
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Daman_and_Diu" title="Daman and Diu">
        Daman and Diu
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the language is still spoken by about 10,000 people. In 2014, an estimated 1,500 students were learning Portuguese in Goa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">
        Official status
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Portuguese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Official status">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_territorial_entities_where_Portuguese_is_an_official_language" title="List of territorial entities where Portuguese is an official language">
        List of territorial entities where Portuguese is an official language
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lusophone_World.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Lusophone_World.svg/450px-Lusophone_World.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Lusophone_World.svg/675px-Lusophone_World.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Lusophone_World.svg/900px-Lusophone_World.svg.png 2x" width="450"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lusophone_World.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Countries and regions where Portuguese has official status.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Community_of_Portuguese_Language_Countries" title="Community of Portuguese Language Countries">
        Community of Portuguese Language Countries
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPLP_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-CPLP-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (in Portuguese
       <i>
        Comunidade dos Países de Língua Portuguesa
       </i>
       , with the Portuguese acronym CPLP) consists of the eight independent countries that have Portuguese as an
       <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">
        official language
       </a>
       :
       <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">
        Angola
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">
        Cape Verde
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/East_Timor" title="East Timor">
        East Timor
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_Guinea" title="Equatorial Guinea">
        Equatorial Guinea
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Guinea-Bissau" title="Guinea-Bissau">
        Guinea-Bissau
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">
        Mozambique
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">
        Portugal
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Tom%C3%A9_and_Pr%C3%ADncipe" title="São Tomé and Príncipe">
        São Tomé and Príncipe
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPLP_5-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-CPLP-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_Guinea" title="Equatorial Guinea">
        Equatorial Guinea
       </a>
       made a formal application for full membership to the CPLP in June 2010, a status given only to states with Portuguese as an official language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2011, Portuguese became its third official language (besides
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and, in July 2014, the country was accepted as a member of the CPLP.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Portuguese is also one of the official languages of the Special Administrative Region of the People's Republic of China of Macau (alongside
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
        Chinese
       </a>
       ) and of several international organizations, including the
       <a href="/wiki/Mercosur" title="Mercosur">
        Mercosur
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_Ibero-American_States" title="Organization of Ibero-American States">
        Organization of Ibero-American States
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_American_Nations" title="Union of South American Nations">
        Union of South American Nations
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_American_States" title="Organization of American States">
        Organization of American States
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">
        African Union
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-africa-union.org_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-africa-union.org-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_West_African_States" title="Economic Community of West African States">
        Economic Community of West African States
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-africa-union.org_46-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-africa-union.org-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_African_Development_Community" title="Southern African Development Community">
        Southern African Development Community
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-africa-union.org_46-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-africa-union.org-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Population_of_countries_and_jurisdictions_of_Portuguese_official_or_co-official_language">
        Population of countries and jurisdictions of Portuguese official or co-official language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">

        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/The_World_Factbook" title="The World Factbook">
        The World Factbook
       </a>
       country population estimates for 2017, the population of each of the ten jurisdictions is as follows (by descending order):

         </sup>
        </th>

         More information
        </th>

         </b>

         <abbr title="circa">
          c.
         </abbr>

          279 million
         </b>

          Community of Portuguese Language Countries
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <small>
        Notes:
       </small>
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        <small>
         Some
         <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">
          linguists
         </a>
         argue that
         <a href="/wiki/Galician_language" title="Galician language">
          Galician
         </a>
         , spoken in
         <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Spain)" title="Galicia (Spain)">
          Galicia
         </a>
         , is merely a dialect of Portuguese rather than an independent language; this would make northwestern
         <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
          Spain
         </a>
         a part of the Portuguese-speaking world.
        </small>
       </li>
       <li>
        <small>
         <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">
          Macau
         </a>
         is not a sovereign nation. It is one of the two
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_Administrative_Region_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Special Administrative Region of the People's Republic of China">
          Special Administrative Regions of the People's Republic of China
         </a>
         (the other being
         <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
          Anglophone
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">
          Hong Kong
         </a>
         , a former
         <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
          British
         </a>
         colony).
        </small>
       </li>
       <li>
        <small>
         <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_Guinea" title="Equatorial Guinea">
          Equatorial Guinea
         </a>
         adopted Portuguese as one of its official languages in 2007, being admitted to CPLP in 2014. The use of the Portuguese language in this country is limited. However, a Portuguese-based creole language,
         <a href="/wiki/Annobonese_Creole" title="Annobonese Creole">
          Annobonese Creole
         </a>
         , is used, mainly on islands of
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annobon" title="Annobon">
          Annobon
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Bioko" title="Bioko">
          Bioko
         </a>
         .
        </small>
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       The combined population of the entire
       <a href="/wiki/Lusophone" title="Lusophone">
        Lusophone
       </a>
       area was estimated at 279 million in July 2017. This number does not include the Lusophone
       <a href="/wiki/Diaspora" title="Diaspora">
        diaspora
       </a>
       , estimated at approximately 10 million people (including 4.5 million Portuguese, 3 million Brazilians, and half a million Cape Verdeans, among others), although it is hard to obtain official accurate numbers of diasporic Portuguese speakers because a significant portion of these citizens are naturalized citizens born outside of Lusophone territory or are children of immigrants, and may have only a basic command of the language. It is also important to note that a large part of the diaspora is a part of the already-counted population of the Portuguese-speaking countries and territories, such as the high number of Brazilian and
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese-speaking_African_countries" title="Portuguese-speaking African countries">
        PALOP
       </a>
       emigrant citizens in Portugal or the high number of Portuguese emigrant citizens in the PALOP and Brazil.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Portuguese language therefore serves more than 250 million people daily, who have direct or indirect legal, juridical and social contact with it, varying from the only language used in any contact, to only education, contact with local or international administration, commerce and services or the simple sight of road signs, public information and advertising in Portuguese.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Portuguese_as_a_foreign_language">
        Portuguese as a foreign language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Portuguese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Portuguese as a foreign language">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Portuguese is a mandatory subject in the school curriculum in
       <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">
        Uruguay
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
        Argentina
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other countries where Portuguese is taught at schools or is being introduced now include
       <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">
        Venezuela
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">
        Zambia
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Republic of the Congo">
        Republic of the Congo
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estadao_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-estadao-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">
        Senegal
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estadao_53-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-estadao-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">
        Namibia
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.namibian.com.na_30-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-www.namibian.com.na-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Swaziland" title="Swaziland">
        Swaziland
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estadao_53-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-estadao-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">
        South Africa
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estadao_53-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-estadao-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Future">
        Future
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Portuguese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Future">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       According to estimates by
       <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">
        UNESCO
       </a>
       , Portuguese is the fastest-growing
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_language" title="European language">
        European language
       </a>
       after
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       and the language has, according to the newspaper
       <i>
        The Portugal News
       </i>
       publishing data given from UNESCO, the highest potential for growth as an international language in
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">
        southern Africa
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">
        South America
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Portugal_News_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-The_Portugal_News-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Portuguese is a globalized language spoken officially on five continents, and as a second language by millions worldwide.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since 1991, when Brazil signed into the economic community of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercosul" title="Mercosul">
        Mercosul
       </a>
       with other South American nations, namely Argentina, Uruguay and Paraguay, Portuguese is either mandatory, or taught, in the schools of those South American countries.
      </p>
      <p>
       Although early in the 21st century, after Macau was ceded to China and Brazilian immigration to Japan slowed down, the use of Portuguese was in decline in Asia, it is once again becoming a language of opportunity there, mostly because of increased diplomatic and financial ties with economically powerful Portuguese-speaking countries (Brazil, Angola, Mozambique, etc.) in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">
        Dialects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Portuguese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Dialects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_dialects" title="Portuguese dialects">
        Portuguese dialects
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_in_the_Americas" title="Portuguese in the Americas">
        Portuguese in the Americas
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sprachen_Osttimors-en.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1354" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Sprachen_Osttimors-en.png/220px-Sprachen_Osttimors-en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Sprachen_Osttimors-en.png/330px-Sprachen_Osttimors-en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Sprachen_Osttimors-en.png/440px-Sprachen_Osttimors-en.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sprachen_Osttimors-en.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Ethnically diverse
         <a href="/wiki/East_Timor" title="East Timor">
          East Timor
         </a>
         has Portuguese as one of its official languages
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        <span lang="pt">
         <i>
          Você
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       is used for educated, formal and colloquial respectful speech in most Portuguese-speaking regions. In the Brazilian state of Rio Grande do Sul,
       <i>
        <span lang="pt">
         <i>
          você
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       is virtually absent from the spoken language. In Portugal, it may be considered disrespectful to treat a stranger as
       <i>
        <span lang="pt">
         <i>
          você
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       , so the pronoun is either replaced by the name of the person (or a title) or it is omitted, since the verbal conjugation allows the distinction between formal and informal treatment. Riograndense (or Gaúcho) Portuguese normally distinguishes formal from informal speech by verbal conjugation. Informal speech employs
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       followed by third person verbs, formal language retains the traditional second person.
      </p>
      <p>
       Conjugation of
       <i>
        <span lang="pt">
         <i>
          tu
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       has three different forms in Brazil (verb "to see":
       <i>
        <span lang="pt">
         <i>
          tu viste?
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       , in the traditional second person,
       <i>
        <span lang="pt">
         <i>
          tu viu?
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       , in the third person, and
       <i>
        <span lang="pt">
         <i>
          tu visse?
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       , in the innovative second person), the conjugation used in the Brazilian states of Pará, Santa Catarina and Maranhão being generally traditional second person, the kind that is used in other Portuguese-speaking countries and learned in Brazilian schools.
      </p>
      <p>
       The predominance of Southeastern-based media products has established
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       as the pronoun of choice for the second person singular in both writing and multimedia communications. However, in the city of Rio de Janeiro, the country's main cultural centre, the usage of
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       has been expanding ever since the end of the 20th century (see,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a linguistic research on the topic in Portuguese), being most frequent among youngsters and a number of studies have also shown an increase in its use in a number of other Brazilian dialects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Segunda_pessoa_no_Brasil_II.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="847" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Segunda_pessoa_no_Brasil_II.png/220px-Segunda_pessoa_no_Brasil_II.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Segunda_pessoa_no_Brasil_II.png/330px-Segunda_pessoa_no_Brasil_II.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Segunda_pessoa_no_Brasil_II.png/440px-Segunda_pessoa_no_Brasil_II.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Segunda_pessoa_no_Brasil_II.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The status of second person pronouns in Brazil. Red indicates near exclusive use of
         <i>
          você
         </i>
         . Mauve indicates decidedly predominant use of
         <i>
          tu
         </i>
         , but with near exclusive third person (
         <i>
          você
         </i>
         -like) verbal conjugation. Brown indicates 50-50 tu/você variation, being
         <i>
          tu
         </i>
         nearly always accompanied by third person (
         <i>
          você
         </i>
         -like) verbal conjugation. Light blue indicates decidedly predominant to near exclusive use of
         <i>
          tu
         </i>
         with reasonable frequency of second person (
         <i>
          tu
         </i>
         -like) verbal conjugation. Yellow indicates balanced você/tu distribution, being
         <i>
          tu
         </i>
         exclusively accompanied by third person (
         <i>
          você
         </i>
         -like) verbal conjugation. Green indicates balanced você/tu distribution,
         <i>
          tu
         </i>
         being predominantly accompanied by third person (
         <i>
          você
         </i>
         -like) verbal conjugation.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Modern Standard
       <a href="/wiki/European_Portuguese" title="European Portuguese">
        European Portuguese
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="pt">
         português padrão
        </span>
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        <span lang="pt">
         português continental
        </span>
       </i>
       ) is based on the Portuguese spoken in the area including and surrounding the cities of
       <a href="/wiki/Coimbra" title="Coimbra">
        Coimbra
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">
        Lisbon
       </a>
       , in central Portugal. Standard European Portuguese is also the preferred standard by the Portuguese-speaking African countries. As such, and despite the fact that its speakers are dispersed around the world, Portuguese has only two dialects used for learning: the European and the Brazilian. Some aspects and sounds found in many dialects of Brazil are exclusive to South America, and cannot be found in Europe. However, the Santomean
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_in_Africa" title="Portuguese in Africa">
        Portuguese in Africa
       </a>
       may be confused with a Brazilian dialect by its phonology and prosody.
      </p>
      <p>
       Audio samples of some dialects and accents of Portuguese are available below.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There are some differences between the areas but these are the best approximations possible. IPA transcriptions refer to the names in local pronunciation.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Portuguese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Brazil">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <ol>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Caipira_dialect" title="Caipira dialect">
          Caipira
         </a>
        </i>
        — Spoken in the states of
        <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state)" title="São Paulo (state)">
         São Paulo
        </a>
        (most markedly on the countryside and rural areas); southern
        <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais" title="Minas Gerais">
         Minas Gerais
        </a>
        , northern
        <a href="/wiki/Paran%C3%A1_(state)" title="Paraná (state)">
         Paraná
        </a>
        and southeastern
        <a href="/wiki/Mato_Grosso_do_Sul" title="Mato Grosso do Sul">
         Mato Grosso do Sul
        </a>
        . Depending on the vision of what constitutes
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Tri%C3%A2ngulo_Mineiro" title="Triângulo Mineiro">
         Triângulo Mineiro
        </a>
        , border areas of
        <a href="/wiki/Goi%C3%A1s" title="Goiás">
         Goiás
        </a>
        and the remaining parts of Mato Grosso do Sul are included, and the frontier of
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        in Minas Gerais is expanded further northerly, though not reaching metropolitan
        <a href="/wiki/Belo_Horizonte" title="Belo Horizonte">
         Belo Horizonte
        </a>
        . It is often said that
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        appeared by
        <a href="/wiki/Decreolization" title="Decreolization">
         decreolization
        </a>
        of the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Tupi" title="Old Tupi">
         língua brasílica
        </a>
        and the related
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L%C3%ADngua_geral_paulista" title="Língua geral paulista">
         língua geral paulista
        </a>
        , then spoken in almost all of what is now São Paulo, a former
        <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
         lingua franca
        </a>
        in most of the contemporary
        <a href="/wiki/Centro-Sul" title="Centro-Sul">
         Centro-Sul
        </a>
        of Brazil before the 18th century, brought by the
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bandeirante" title="Bandeirante">
          bandeirantes
         </a>
        </i>
        , interior pioneers of
        <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Brazil" title="Colonial Brazil">
         Colonial Brazil
        </a>
        , closely related to its northern counterpart
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nheengatu_language" title="Nheengatu language">
         Nheengatu
        </a>
        , and that is why the dialect shows many general differences from other variants of the language.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
         <a href="#cite_note-60">
          [60]
         </a>
        </sup>
        It has striking remarkable differences in comparison to other Brazilian dialects in phonology, prosody and grammar, often
        <a href="/wiki/Social_stigma" title="Social stigma">
         stigmatized
        </a>
        as being strongly associated with a
        <a href="/wiki/Prestige_(sociolinguistics)" title="Prestige (sociolinguistics)">
         substandard variant
        </a>
        , now mostly rural.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
         <a href="#cite_note-61">
          [61]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
         <a href="#cite_note-62">
          [62]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
         <a href="#cite_note-63">
          [63]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
         <a href="#cite_note-64">
          [64]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
         <a href="#cite_note-65">
          [65]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/North_coast_Portuguese" title="North coast Portuguese">
         <i>
          Cearense
         </i>
         or
         <i>
          Costa norte
         </i>
        </a>
        — is a dialect spoken more sharply in the states of Ceará and Piauí. The variant of Ceará includes fairly distinctive traits it shares with the one spoken in Piauí, though, such as distinctive regional phonology and vocabulary (for example, a debuccalization process stronger than that of Portuguese, a different system of the vowel harmony that spans Brazil from
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         mineiro
        </i>
        to
        <i>
         amazofonia
        </i>
        but is especially prevalent in
        <i>
         nordestino
        </i>
        , a very coherent coda sibilant palatalization as those of Portugal and Rio de Janeiro but allowed in fewer environments than in other accents of
        <i>
         nordestino
        </i>
        , a greater presence of dental stop palatalization to palato-alveolar in comparison to other accents of
        <i>
         nordestino
        </i>
        , among others, as well as a great number of archaic Portuguese words).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
         <a href="#cite_note-66">
          [66]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
         <a href="#cite_note-67">
          [67]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
         <a href="#cite_note-68">
          [68]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
         <a href="#cite_note-69">
          [69]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
         <a href="#cite_note-70">
          [70]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
         <a href="#cite_note-71">
          [71]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Baiano
        </i>
        — Found in
        <a href="/wiki/Bahia" title="Bahia">
         Bahia
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Sergipe" title="Sergipe">
         Sergipe
        </a>
        , northern Minas Gerais and border regions with
        <a href="/wiki/Goi%C3%A1s" title="Goiás">
         Goiás
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Tocantins" title="Tocantins">
         Tocantins
        </a>
        . Similar to
        <i>
         nordestino
        </i>
        , it has a very characteristic
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_timing" title="Stress timing">
         syllable-timed rhythm
        </a>
        and the greatest tendency to pronounce unstressed vowels as open-mid
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">
          ɛ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid back rounded vowel">
          ɔ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        .
        <div class="thumb tright">
         <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png">
           <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="915" data-file-width="885" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png/220px-Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png/330px-Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png/440px-Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png 2x" width="220"/>
          </a>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <div class="magnify">
            <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png" title="Enlarge">
            </a>
           </div>
           <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">
            Variants
           </a>
           and
           <a href="/wiki/Sociolect" title="Sociolect">
            sociolects
           </a>
           of Brazilian Portuguese.
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loudspeaker.svg">
         <img alt="Loudspeaker.svg" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
        </a>
        <a class="external text" href="http://cvc.instituto-camoes.pt/hlp/geografia/som90.html" rel="nofollow">
         <i>
          Fluminense
         </i>
        </a>
        — A broad dialect with many variants spoken in the states of
        <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro_(state)" title="Rio de Janeiro (state)">
         Rio de Janeiro
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Esp%C3%ADrito_Santo" title="Espírito Santo">
         Espírito Santo
        </a>
        and neighbouring eastern regions of
        <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais" title="Minas Gerais">
         Minas Gerais
        </a>
        .
        <i>
         Fluminense
        </i>
        formed in these previously
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        -speaking areas due to the gradual influence of European migrants, causing many people to distance their speech from their original dialect and incorporate new terms.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
         <a href="#cite_note-72">
          [72]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <i>
         Fluminense
        </i>
        is sometimes referred to as
        <i>
         carioca
        </i>
        , however
        <i>
         carioca
        </i>
        is a more specific term referring to the accent of the
        <a href="/wiki/Greater_Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Greater Rio de Janeiro">
         Greater Rio de Janeiro
        </a>
        area by speakers with a
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        dialect.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Gaúcho
        </i>
        — in
        <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_do_Sul" title="Rio Grande do Sul">
         Rio Grande do Sul
        </a>
        , similar to
        <i>
         sulista
        </i>
        . There are many distinct accents in Rio Grande do Sul, mainly due to the heavy influx of European immigrants of diverse origins who have settled in colonies throughout the state, and to the proximity to
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hispanosphere" title="Hispanosphere">
         Spanish-speaking nations
        </a>
        . The
        <i>
         gaúcho
        </i>
        word in itself is a Spanish
        <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
         loanword
        </a>
        into Portuguese of obscure
        <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_languages_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous languages of the Americas">
         Indigenous Amerindian
        </a>
        origins.
        <div class="thumb tright">
         <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Falantes_no_mundo.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="408" data-file-width="513" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Falantes_no_mundo.jpg/200px-Falantes_no_mundo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Falantes_no_mundo.jpg/300px-Falantes_no_mundo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Falantes_no_mundo.jpg/400px-Falantes_no_mundo.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
          </a>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <div class="magnify">
            <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Falantes_no_mundo.jpg" title="Enlarge">
            </a>
           </div>
           Percentage of worldwide Portuguese speakers per country.
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Mineiro" title="Mineiro">
          Mineiro
         </a>
        </i>
        —
        <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais" title="Minas Gerais">
         Minas Gerais
        </a>
        (not prevalent in the
        <a href="/wiki/Tri%C3%A2ngulo_Mineiro" title="Triângulo Mineiro">
         Triângulo Mineiro
        </a>
        ). As the
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        area, its associated region was formerly a sparsely populated land where
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        was spoken, but
        <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais#History" title="Minas Gerais">
         the discovery of gold and gems made it the most prosperous Brazilian region
        </a>
        , what attracted Portuguese colonists, commoners from other parts of Brazil and their African slaves. South-southwestern,
        <a href="/wiki/Zona_da_Mata_(Minas_Gerais)" title="Zona da Mata (Minas Gerais)">
         southeastern
        </a>
        and northern areas of the state have fairly distinctive speech, actually approximating to
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        (popularly called, often pejoratively,
        <i>
         carioca do brejo
        </i>
        , "marsh carioca") and
        <i>
         baiano
        </i>
        respectively. Areas including and surrounding
        <a href="/wiki/Belo_Horizonte" title="Belo Horizonte">
         Belo Horizonte
        </a>
        have a distinctive accent.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loudspeaker.svg">
         <img alt="Loudspeaker.svg" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
        </a>
        <a class="external text" href="http://cvc.instituto-camoes.pt/hlp/geografia/som91.html" rel="nofollow">
         <i>
          Nordestino
         </i>
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_73-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-73">
          [73]
         </a>
        </sup>
        — more marked in the
        <a href="/wiki/Sert%C3%A3o" title="Sertão">
         Sertão
        </a>
        (7), where, in the 19th and 20th centuries and especially in the area including and surrounding the
        <i>
         sertão
        </i>
        (the dry land after
        <a href="/wiki/Agreste" title="Agreste">
         Agreste
        </a>
        ) of Pernambuco and southern Ceará, it could sound less comprehensible to speakers of other Portuguese dialects than Galician or
        <a href="/wiki/Rioplatense_Spanish" title="Rioplatense Spanish">
         Rioplatense Spanish
        </a>
        , and nowadays less distinctive from other variants in the metropolitan cities
        <a href="/wiki/Zona_da_Mata" title="Zona da Mata">
         along the coasts
        </a>
        . It can be divided in two regional variants, one that includes the northern
        <a href="/wiki/Maranh%C3%A3o" title="Maranhão">
         Maranhão
        </a>
        and southern of
        <a href="/wiki/Piau%C3%AD" title="Piauí">
         Piauí
        </a>
        , and other that goes from
        <a href="/wiki/Cear%C3%A1" title="Ceará">
         Ceará
        </a>
        to
        <a href="/wiki/Alagoas" title="Alagoas">
         Alagoas
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Nortista
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         amazofonia
        </i>
        — Most of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_Basin" title="Amazon Basin">
         Amazon Basin
        </a>
        states i.e.
        <a href="/wiki/North_Region,_Brazil" title="North Region, Brazil">
         Northern Brazil
        </a>
        . Before the 20th century, most people from the
        <i>
         nordestino
        </i>
        area fleeing the droughts and their associated poverty settled here, so it has some similarities with the Portuguese dialect there spoken. The speech in and around the cities of
        <a href="/wiki/Bel%C3%A9m" title="Belém">
         Belém
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Manaus" title="Manaus">
         Manaus
        </a>
        has a more European flavor in phonology, prosody and grammar.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Paulistano
        </i>
        — Variants spoken around
        <a href="/wiki/Greater_S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="Greater São Paulo">
         Greater São Paulo
        </a>
        in its maximum definition and more easterly areas of São Paulo state, as well perhaps "educated speech" from anywhere in
        <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state)" title="São Paulo (state)">
         the state of São Paulo
        </a>
        (where it coexists with
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        ).
        <i>
         Caipira
        </i>
        is the hinterland sociolect of much of the
        <a href="/wiki/Centro-Sul" title="Centro-Sul">
         Central-Southern half
        </a>
        of Brazil, nowadays conservative only in the rural areas and associated with them, that has a historically
        <a href="/wiki/Prestige_(sociolinguistics)" title="Prestige (sociolinguistics)">
         low prestige
        </a>
        in cities as Rio de Janeiro, Curitiba, Belo Horizonte, and until some years ago, in São Paulo itself.
        <a href="/wiki/Sociolinguistics" title="Sociolinguistics">
         Sociolinguistics
        </a>
        , or what by times is described as '
        <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_discrimination" title="Linguistic discrimination">
         linguistic prejudice
        </a>
        ', often correlated with
        <a href="/wiki/Class_discrimination" title="Class discrimination">
         classism
        </a>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
         <a href="#cite_note-74">
          [74]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
         <a href="#cite_note-75">
          [75]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
         <a href="#cite_note-76">
          [76]
         </a>
        </sup>
        is a polemic topic in the entirety of the country since the times of
        <a href="/wiki/Adoniran_Barbosa#Musical_production" title="Adoniran Barbosa">
         Adoniran Barbosa
        </a>
        . Also, the "Paulistano" accent was heavily influenced by the presence of immigrants in the city of São Paulo, especially the Italians.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Sertanejo
        </i>
        —
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center-West_Region,_Brazil" title="Center-West Region, Brazil">
         Center-Western states
        </a>
        , and also much of
        <a href="/wiki/Tocantins" title="Tocantins">
         Tocantins
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Rond%C3%B4nia" title="Rondônia">
         Rondônia
        </a>
        . It is closer to
        <i>
         mineiro
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         nordestino
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         nortista
        </i>
        depending on the location.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Sulista
        </i>
        — The variants spoken in the areas between the northern regions of
        <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_do_Sul" title="Rio Grande do Sul">
         Rio Grande do Sul
        </a>
        and southern regions of São Paulo state, encompassing most of
        <a href="/wiki/South_Region,_Brazil" title="South Region, Brazil">
         southern Brazil
        </a>
        . The city of
        <a href="/wiki/Curitiba" title="Curitiba">
         Curitiba
        </a>
        does have a fairly distinct accent as well, and a relative majority of speakers around and in
        <a href="/wiki/Florian%C3%B3polis" title="Florianópolis">
         Florianópolis
        </a>
        also speak this variant (many speak
        <i>
         florianopolitano
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         manezinho da ilha
        </i>
        instead, related to the European Portuguese dialects spoken in
        <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">
         Azores
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">
         Madeira
        </a>
        ). Speech of northern Paraná is closer to that of inland São Paulo.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Florianopolitan_dialect" title="Florianopolitan dialect">
          Florianopolitano
         </a>
        </i>
        — Variants heavily influenced by European Portuguese spoken in
        <a href="/wiki/Florian%C3%B3polis" title="Florianópolis">
         Florianópolis
        </a>
        city (due to a heavy immigration movement from Portugal, mainly its
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autonomous_regions_of_Portugal" title="Autonomous regions of Portugal">
         insular regions
        </a>
        ) and much of its metropolitan area,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grande_Florian%C3%B3polis" title="Grande Florianópolis">
         Grande Florianópolis
        </a>
        , said to be a continuum between those whose speech most resemble
        <i>
         sulista
        </i>
        dialects and those whose speech most resemble
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        and European ones, called, often pejoratively,
        <i>
         manezinho da ilha
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Carioca" title="Carioca">
          Carioca
         </a>
        </i>
        — Not a dialect, but
        <a href="/wiki/Sociolect" title="Sociolect">
         sociolects
        </a>
        of the
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        variant spoken in an area roughly corresponding to
        <a href="/wiki/Greater_Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Greater Rio de Janeiro">
         Greater Rio de Janeiro
        </a>
        . It appeared after locals came in contact with the Portuguese aristocracy amidst the
        <a href="/wiki/Transfer_of_the_Portuguese_Court_to_Brazil" title="Transfer of the Portuguese Court to Brazil">
         Portuguese royal family fled
        </a>
        in the early 19th century. There is actually a continuum between Vernacular countryside accents and the
        <i>
         carioca
        </i>
        sociolect, and the educated speech (in Portuguese
        <i>
         norma culta
        </i>
        , which most closely resembles other Brazilian Portuguese standards but with marked recent Portuguese influences, the nearest ones among the country's dialects along
        <i>
         florianopolitano
        </i>
        ), so that not all people native to the state of Rio de Janeiro speak the said sociolect, but most
        <i>
         carioca
        </i>
        speakers will use the standard variant not influenced by it that is rather uniform around Brazil depending on context (emphasis or formality, for example).
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Brasiliense
        </i>
        — used in
        <a href="/wiki/Bras%C3%ADlia" title="Brasília">
         Brasília
        </a>
        and its metropolitan area.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
         <a href="#cite_note-77">
          [77]
         </a>
        </sup>
        It is not considered a dialect, but more of a regional variant – often deemed to be closer to
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        than the dialect commonly spoken in most of Goiás,
        <i>
         sertanejo
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Arco do desflorestamento
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://pt.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dialeto_da_serra_amaz%C3%B4nica" title="pt:Dialeto da serra amazônica">
          serra amazônica
         </a>
        </i>
        — Known in its region as the "accent of the migrants", it has similarities with
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         sertanejo
        </i>
        and often
        <i>
         sulista
        </i>
        that make it differing from
        <i>
         amazofonia
        </i>
        (in the opposite group of Brazilian dialects, in which it is placed along
        <i>
         nordestino
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         baiano
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         mineiro
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        ). It is the most recent dialect, which appeared by the settlement of families from various other Brazilian regions attracted by the cheap land offer in recently
        <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">
         deforested
        </a>
        areas.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
         <a href="#cite_note-78">
          [78]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
         <a href="#cite_note-79">
          [79]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Recifense
        </i>
        — used in
        <a href="/wiki/Recife" title="Recife">
         Recife
        </a>
        and its metropolitan area.
       </li>
      </ol>

       <span class="mw-headline" id="Portugal">

       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">

        </a>

        </span>
       </span>

       </li>

         <i>

         </i>
        </a>
        —
        <a href="/wiki/Alentejo" title="Alentejo">

        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Alentejan_Portuguese" title="Alentejan Portuguese">

        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loudspeaker.svg">
